<div id="review_3260596419" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3260596419" />
    <a title="Brittney Gibbon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28291083-brittney-gibbon"><img alt="Brittney Gibbon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582627143p2/28291083.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3260596419?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brittney Gibbon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brittney Gibbon" href="/user/show/28291083-brittney-gibbon">Brittney Gibbon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781784700553" href="/book/show/30965675-the-blade-artist">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3260596419" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1077647520903922372"><br/>“‘You’ve nae emotional connection to anybody!’<br/>‘Anger is an emotion,’ Franco says, opening the door and exiting.”<br/><br/>And that is the Franco Begbie that I have known for the last 13 or so years; an angry, unnecessarily violent thug, with a warped moral centre and little to no regard for those around him.<br/><br/>How things change.<br/><br/>Don’t get me wrong, Bebgie is still an angry human, but these days he is channelling that rage into his art and has implemented measures to keep himself in check; staying off the pish</span>
  <span id="freeText1077647520903922372" style="display:none"><br />“‘You’ve nae emotional connection to anybody!’<br />‘Anger is an emotion,’ Franco says, opening the door and exiting.”<br /><br />And that is the Franco Begbie that I have known for the last 13 or so years; an angry, unnecessarily violent thug, with a warped moral centre and little to no regard for those around him.<br /><br />How things change.<br /><br />Don’t get me wrong, Bebgie is still an angry human, but these days he is channelling that rage into his art and has implemented measures to keep himself in check; staying off the pish, genuinely caring for his wife and daughters, a quick name change and a couple of key breathing exercises seem to be helping a lot.<br /><br />The Blade Artist offers an insight into a side of Francis Begbie that I didn’t even realise I was dying for. Flitting between his new life in California, the goings on once he heads back to Scotland, and flashbacks to his past (including memories of some favourite characters 🖤), Welsh draws a stark contrast between the two worlds and you’d almost be forgiven for blaming Edinburgh for every single poor decision Bebgie has made.<br /><br />The flashbacks to Begbie’s earlier years help to really understand where the chip on his shoulder came from, the snippets of his life in California show the man he is capable of being, and his time spent back in Scotland highlights that the man will never really change – the only difference is now his moral centre might be a little more on track.<br /><br />As usual, Welsh doesn’t shy away from the grim reality of life in this kind of circle, nor is he afraid to litter the pages with violence, drugs, drinking and his warped sense of humour. The Blade Artist is funny, it is tense, it has shocks and twists that had me had me head in hands groaning into an empty room “oh come on, now what?!” and it has moments of real, surprising emotion all driven by a theme of family and obligation.<br /><br />Welsh is my favourite author for a reason and The Blade Artist is a perfect example of why.<br /><br />“‘That’s ma name,’ Begbie admits, with a dry chortle. Jim has a nice life, he considers, but sometimes Frank has a hell of a lot more fun.”<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1077647520903922372" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1647446631" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1647446631" />
    <a title="Aaron Nash" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45707654-aaron-nash"><img alt="Aaron Nash" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1511167493p2/45707654.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1647446631?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aaron Nash" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aaron Nash" href="/user/show/45707654-aaron-nash">Aaron Nash</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1647446631" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12478878358420174505">I've waited a long time for this book. Fans of Welsh, I expect, feel the same. A book based all around the life of Francis Begbie, one of Welshs most famous characters. What could possible go wrong? Well a few things actually.<br/><br/>Let's be honest we all wanted a tale of the Begbie we all know and love. The ultra violent, psycopath whom his friends and enemies both fear. The guy who if you saw in a bar you would walk out. The guy who, if you said one wrong word too, wouldn't think twice about glassing</span>
  <span id="freeText12478878358420174505" style="display:none">I've waited a long time for this book. Fans of Welsh, I expect, feel the same. A book based all around the life of Francis Begbie, one of Welshs most famous characters. What could possible go wrong? Well a few things actually.<br /><br />Let's be honest we all wanted a tale of the Begbie we all know and love. The ultra violent, psycopath whom his friends and enemies both fear. The guy who if you saw in a bar you would walk out. The guy who, if you said one wrong word too, wouldn't think twice about glassing you for being a "wide cunt." Instead we get a tale of redemption. Sort of ...<br /><br />The begbie we all know and love is gone, replaced by a family man. He now resides in L.A, a successful artist, sober and genuinely living a carefree life. After hearing about the death of his son, he has to return to Scotland, the place he left his demons and everything that shaped his previous life behind. Here he is confronted by his family, his old friends and previous employees, each of them not believing for one second this "front" that Begbie is now putting on. Is it a front though? Or has Begbie really changed? That is what the novel slowly builds towards as Begbie is constantly tested by characters from his previous life; getting wound up, being goaded into fights, signs of his old accent returning, whilst all the while we wait for the guy we all know and love to show his real face. It is a credit then that I really was not sure whether this was going to happen. Does it? Well that would be telling. <br /><br />The problem I had though was that after reading Begbie in Welshs previous novels, a tale of redemption for him is a little bit ridiculous. This character was horror personified. He had no redeeming qualities whatsoever. He loved the ultra violent lifestyle he led and that was never going to change. Also lacking from the novel was Welshs trademark humour. The previous works that begbie has appeared in I used to look forward to the chapters he appeared in, or the chapters from his point of view, because I knew that they ere going to be dark and hilarious. This is the novel that I wanted from a story that was solely from his point of view. Unfortunately it wasn't the story that we got. <br /><br />Verdict : Begbie is back! Unfortunately not as we know him. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12478878358420174505" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1594886596" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1594886596" />
    <a title="Sannie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1794595-sannie"><img alt="Sannie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514119227p2/1794595.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1594886596?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sannie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sannie" href="/user/show/1794595-sannie">Sannie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781784700553" href="/book/show/30965675-the-blade-artist">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Irvine Welsh fans</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1594886596" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14702968674110996587">This is probably my least favorite novel in all of the <i>Trainspotting</i> books. Focusing on Frank Begbie and how he's turned his life around in the United States, it's perhaps more readable if only because Irvine Welsh writes in standard English. However, this is actually the charm about reading any of the <i>Trainspotting</i> books and it doesn't really feel like it belongs to the other books.<br/><br/>But maybe that's the point. Begbie has left his past in Edinburgh behind, becoming an artist in California, marrie</span>
  <span id="freeText14702968674110996587" style="display:none">This is probably my least favorite novel in all of the <i>Trainspotting</i> books. Focusing on Frank Begbie and how he's turned his life around in the United States, it's perhaps more readable if only because Irvine Welsh writes in standard English. However, this is actually the charm about reading any of the <i>Trainspotting</i> books and it doesn't really feel like it belongs to the other books.<br /><br />But maybe that's the point. Begbie has left his past in Edinburgh behind, becoming an artist in California, married with two daughters. The fact that it <i>isn't</i> in Scottish English makes the change seem real, but we see Begbie slip back into the Scottish dialect. It's an effective literary tool and Irvine Welsh uses it really well. As much as I missed it from the previous novels, it actually makes sense.<br /><br />We think that Begbie has changed and has his anger under control and that's exactly the problem: instead of being in the crazy predictable psycho that would have raging outbursts on complete strangers, we get a crazy psycho whose rage is controlled and hidden, which might actually be more terrifying.<br /><br />In typical fashion with the previous novels, Begbie is still the anti-hero. We want him so much to succeed in leaving his violent past behind, and we want to believe that he is a loving, caring, protective father and husband. He hasn't left it behind, but he is that figure within his family. This is what makes <i>The Blade Artist</i> a good read, but not one of my favorites in the series.<br /><br />And now moving on to <i>Dead Men's Trousers</i>...</span>
  <a data-text-id="14702968674110996587" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1778457173?book_show_action=true">Nov 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pan Radek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pan Radek" href="/user/show/52776165-pan-radek">Pan Radek</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1778457173" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17368640996201751360">Frank deserves better... <br/><br/><br/>Well there's the case of supporting character who's so interesting/popular that becomes the main character and then we find out they were not as interesting as to become the main forces of a novel /movie - think the Mignions, Hannibal Lecter, the Cleveland Show and the likes <br/><br/>this however is not the case <br/><br/>by all means the plot of the novel is cliché - let's suppose it's called Count Dracula's Big Vacation, all right? our count gave up drinking blood, lives as a respected r</span>
  <span id="freeText17368640996201751360" style="display:none">Frank deserves better... <br /><br /><br />Well there's the case of supporting character who's so interesting/popular that becomes the main character and then we find out they were not as interesting as to become the main forces of a novel /movie - think the Mignions, Hannibal Lecter, the Cleveland Show and the likes <br /><br />this however is not the case <br /><br />by all means the plot of the novel is cliché - let's suppose it's called Count Dracula's Big Vacation, all right? our count gave up drinking blood, lives as a respected retired navy officer in Brighton and regularly attends the local chess club. while on vacation in Hungary he unexpectedly finds himself in good old Transylvania - will he stay to his new path of righteousness of will his true self reemerge in the "no way out" situation - the reader counts on the latter of course and the introduction looks really promising. <br /><br />it is only 3/4 through the book that we realize Irvine Welsh different not have many ideas what to do with Frank exactly and despite some good dialogues and very good characters the novel was unfortunately a disappointment. <br /><br />don't get me wrong I loved Trainspotting and Filth and really liked Porno, this however is out of that league, pity.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('MuX5LE/TMXtQleWbkrtcsdrhK4yLWUZaF1MIIeKcldQIw5D+9I34E/e+UfplTlZuDL7MREXUyD1MLU6QL99nTg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('0N+81lg4ZDPLt8CktdW8CMUhpErPe5dWadbOs9iQLKPq+dUE42atW2ycdMVCILbXE35DggH2GTEyqIgCFdPeOQ==')})
  }
//]]>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3653402917536644476">Probably most famous for his gritty depiction of a gang of Scottish Heroin addicts, <i>Trainspotting</i> (1993), Welsh focuses on the darker side of human nature and drug use. All of his novels are set in his native Scotland and filled with anti-heroes, small time crooks and hooligans. Welsh manages, however to imbue these characters with a sad humanity that makes them likable despite their obvious scumb</span>
  <span id="freeText3653402917536644476" style="display:none">Probably most famous for his gritty depiction of a gang of Scottish Heroin addicts, <i>Trainspotting</i> (1993), Welsh focuses on the darker side of human nature and drug use. All of his novels are set in his native Scotland and filled with anti-heroes, small time crooks and hooligans. Welsh manages, however to imbue these characters with a sad humanity that makes them likable despite their obvious scumbaggerry. Irvine Welsh is also known for writing in his native Edinburgh Scots dialect, making his prose challenging for the average reader unfamiliar with this style.</span>

He’s then rocked by a chance encounter with Frank Begbie, from whom he’d been hiding for years after a terrible betrayal and the resulting debt. But the psychotic Begbie appears to have reinvented himself as a celebrated artist and - much to Mark’s astonishment - doesn’t seem interested in revenge.\n\nSick Boy and Spud, who have agendas of their own, are intrigued to learn that their old friends are back in town, but when they enter the bleak world of organ-harvesting, things start to go so badly wrong. Lurching from crisis to crisis, the four men circle each other, driven by their personal histories and addictions, confused, angry - so desperate that even Hibs winning the Scottish Cup doesn’t really help. One of these four will not survive to the end of this book. It isn't just that the place he now resides in and the people around him are poles apart, it's like he himself is an entirely different person. The overriding obsessions and foibles of the man he'd once been now feel utterly ludicrous to the current resident of his mind and body. The only bridge is rage; when angered he can taste his old self. But in California, the way he is currently living his life, few things can vex him to that extent. But that's over there.&rdquo;</span>

  if (w.attachEvent) {
    w.attachEvent('onload', go);
  } else {
    w.addEventListener('load', go, false);
  }
}(window, document,
    ['//assets.pinterest.com/js/pinit.js',
     '//apis.google.com/js/plusone.js'
    ]
  ));

//]]>
</script>
